	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Civil parishes in West Yorkshire</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=List_of_civil_parishes_in_West_Yorkshire&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="List of civil parishes in West Yorkshire">List of civil parishes in West Yorkshire</a>)</span></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EnglandWestYorkshireNumbered.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ea/EnglandWestYorkshireNumbered.png" decoding="async" width="200" height="157" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="157" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EnglandWestYorkshireNumbered.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>, showing the Metropolitan Boroughs: (1) <a href="/wiki/Leeds" title="Leeds">Leeds</a>; (2) <a href="/wiki/City_of_Wakefield" title="City of Wakefield">Wakefield</a>; (3) <a href="/wiki/Kirklees" title="Kirklees">Kirklees</a>; (4) <a href="/wiki/Calderdale" title="Calderdale">Calderdale</a>; and (5) <a href="/wiki/City_of_Bradford" title="City of Bradford">Bradford</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Civil_parishes_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil parishes in England">civil parish</a> is a subnational entity, forming the lowest unit of <a href="/wiki/Local_government_in_England" title="Local government in England">local government</a> in <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>. There are 97 civil parishes in the <a href="/wiki/Ceremonial_county" class="mw-redirect" title="Ceremonial county">ceremonial county</a> of <a href="/wiki/West_Yorkshire" title="West Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a>, most of the county being <a href="/wiki/Unparished_area" title="Unparished area">unparished</a>.  At the 2001 census, there were 557,369 people living in the 97 parishes, accounting for 26.8 per cent of the county's population.
</p>
<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" lang="en" dir="ltr"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_parishes_in_West_Yorkshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Parishes arose from <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a> divisions, and were originally purely ecclesiastical divisions.  Over time they acquired civil administration powers.<sup id="cite_ref-anguswinchester_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-anguswinchester-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Highways_Act_1555" title="Highways Act 1555">Highways Act 1555</a> made parishes responsible for the upkeep of roads. Every adult inhabitant of the parish was obliged to work four days a year on the roads, providing their own tools, carts and horses; the work was overseen by an unpaid local appointee, the <i>Surveyor of Highways</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The poor were looked after by the <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a>, until their <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Monasteries" title="Dissolution of the Monasteries">dissolution</a>. In 1572, magistrates were given power to 'survey the poor' and impose taxes for their relief.  This system was made more formal by the <a href="/wiki/Elizabethan_Poor_Law_(1601)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elizabethan Poor Law (1601)">Poor Law Act 1601</a>, which made parishes responsible for administering the <a href="/wiki/English_Poor_Laws" title="English Poor Laws">Poor Law</a>; overseers were appointed to charge a <a href="/wiki/Rates_(tax)" title="Rates (tax)">rate</a> to support the poor of the parish.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>  The 19th century saw an increase in the responsibility of parishes, although the <i>Poor Law</i> powers were transferred to <a href="/wiki/Poor_Law_Unions" class="mw-redirect" title="Poor Law Unions">Poor Law Unions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Sanitary_district" title="Sanitary district">Public Health Act 1872</a> grouped parishes into Rural Sanitary Districts, based on the Poor Law Unions; these subsequently formed the basis for <a href="/wiki/Rural_District" class="mw-redirect" title="Rural District">Rural Districts</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Parishes were run by <a href="/wiki/Vestry" title="Vestry">vestries</a>, meeting annually to appoint officials, and were generally identical to ecclesiastical parishes,<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> although some <a href="/wiki/Township_(England)" title="Township (England)">townships</a> in large parishes administered the <i>Poor Law</i> themselves; under the <a href="/wiki/Divided_Parishes_and_Poor_Law_Amendment_Act_1882" title="Divided Parishes and Poor Law Amendment Act 1882">Divided Parishes and Poor Law Amendment Act 1882</a>, all <a href="/wiki/Extra-parochial_area" title="Extra-parochial area">extra-parochial areas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Township_(England)" title="Township (England)">townships</a> that levied a separate rate became independent civil parishes.<sup id="cite_ref-surname_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surname-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Civil parishes in their modern sense date from the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1894" title="Local Government Act 1894">Local Government Act 1894</a>, which abolished <a href="/wiki/Vestry" title="Vestry">vestries</a>; established elected parish councils in all rural parishes with more than 300 electors; grouped rural parishes into Rural Districts; and aligned parish boundaries with county and borough boundaries.<sup id="cite_ref-surname_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-surname-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>  Urban civil parishes continued to exist, and were generally coterminous with the <a href="/wiki/Urban_district_(Great_Britain_and_Ireland)" title="Urban district (Great Britain and Ireland)">Urban District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Borough" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipal Borough">Municipal Borough</a> or <a href="/wiki/County_Borough" class="mw-redirect" title="County Borough">County Borough</a> in which they were situated; many large towns contained a number of parishes, and these were usually merged into one.  Parish councils were not formed in urban areas, and the only function of the parish was to elect guardians to Poor Law Unions; with the abolition of the <i>Poor Law</i> system in 1930 the parishes had only a nominal existence.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1972" title="Local Government Act 1972">Local Government Act 1972</a> retained civil parishes in rural areas, and many former <a href="/wiki/Urban_district_(Great_Britain_and_Ireland)" title="Urban district (Great Britain and Ireland)">Urban Districts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Borough" class="mw-redirect" title="Municipal Borough">Municipal Boroughs</a> that were being abolished, were replaced by new successor parishes; urban areas that were considered too large to be single parishes became <a href="/wiki/Unparished_area" title="Unparished area">unparished areas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-act_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-act-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="center"><div class="thumb tnone"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Yorkshire_County.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/West_Yorkshire_County.png/500px-West_Yorkshire_County.png" decoding="async" width="500" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/West_Yorkshire_County.png/750px-West_Yorkshire_County.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/West_Yorkshire_County.png/1000px-West_Yorkshire_County.png 2x" data-file-width="3276" data-file-height="2106" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:West_Yorkshire_County.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>West Yorkshire showing the former local authorities</div></div></div></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_current_position">The current position</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Civil_parishes_in_West_Yorkshire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: The current position">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Recent governments have encouraged the formation of town and parish councils in unparished areas, and the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_and_Rating_Act_1997" title="Local Government and Rating Act 1997">Local Government and Rating Act 1997</a> gave local residents the right to demand the creation of a new civil parish.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A parish council can become a town council unilaterally, simply by resolution;<sup id="cite_ref-act_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-act-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and a civil parish can also gain <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city status</a>, but only if that is granted by <a href="/wiki/British_monarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="British monarchy">the Crown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-act_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-act-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  The chairman of a town or city council is called a <a href="/wiki/Mayors_in_England" title="Mayors in England">mayor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-act_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-act-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>  The <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_and_Public_Involvement_in_Health_Act_2007" title="Local Government and Public Involvement in Health Act 2007">Local Government and Public Involvement in Health Act 2007</a> introduced alternative names: a parish council can now choose to be called a community; village; or neighbourhood council.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
